state.\n\nLegislative Bill 75, from State Sen. Mike McDonnell of Omaha, would exempt qualified Nebraska homeowners from paying any property taxes after 40 years of ownership in the state.\n\n\"I think you should be able to sit on your porch one day of your life and say, 'I truly own my home, and I'm no longer a partner in paying rent t"}, "location": {"type": "place", "label": {"eng": "Nebraska"}, "country": {"type": "country", "label": {"eng": "United States"}}}, "categories": [{"uri": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Business", "label": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Business", "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Business/Accounting/Tax_Negotiation_and_Representation", "label": "dmoz/Business/Accounting/Tax Negotiation and Representation", "wgt": 74}, {"uri": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Labor", "label": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Labor", "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "news/Business", "label": "news/Business", "wgt": 88}], "articleCounts": {"eng": 25}, "sentiment": 0.02745098039215677}
{"uri": "8249273279", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "16:43:22", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T16:43:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T16:42:43Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.843137264251709, "url": "https://journalstar.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/nebraska-legislature-reassigns-pillens-property-tax-plan-to-revenue-committee/article_c6f34776-4dbc-11ef-a911-b7b0a1f2e2ec.html", "title": "Nebraska Legislature reassigns Pillen's property tax plan to Revenue Committee", "body": "Legislature public hearings; Challenge to LB574 rejected;Library Board challenge\n\nGov. Jim Pillen's plan to cut property taxes will go before the Revenue Committee later this week after lawmakers voted on Monday to change its committee assignment.\n\nThe Legislature's Referencing Committee voted 6-3 to move LB1 out of the Government, Military and Veterans Affairs Committee, where it was assigned in a surprise move last week, and into the Revenue Committee.\n\nIn the committee led by Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of Elkhorn, who introduced LB1 on Pillen's behalf on the first day of a special session called by the governor, the bill will likely have an easier path to the floor of the Legislature.\n\nThe move also gives any amendments proposed by the Revenue Committee priority when the bill comes up for debate by the full Legislature.\n\nPeople are also reading... 'Ready to learn from this': John Cook updates Harper Murray's status for 2024 season Judge orders Nebraska Department of Transportation to turn over records of intersection study Time, TV for Jordan Larson and the US women's volleyball team at the Paris Olympics 'Exhausting, heartbreaking, devastating': Racism at Wayne school pushes family out of Nebraska Nebraska Gov. Pillen would reduce own property tax bill nearly $1M if cuts pass 7 killed after plane departing Nebraska City crashes in Wyoming, including members of gospel group In a split decision, Nebraska Supreme Court rejects legal challenge to abortion, trans care ban 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street LPS board considering selling south Lincoln land for city park; more vape detectors bought Notes: Ron Brown gets new title; Matt Rhule on Nebraska's place in national NIL scene Nearly 100 Lincoln Industries employees summit mountains in Colorado Nebraska lawmakers begin taking sides as property tax special session continues Kearney man runs American flag upside down as 'distress' signal On eve of special session, Nebraska Gov. Jim Pillen defends tax plan, personal gain Nebraska's Ty Robinson, Lincoln company prove clever NIL deals do still exist\n\nSen. Ray Aguilar of Grand Island, the chairman of the Executive Board, which also serves as the Referencing Committee, and Sen. John Lowe of Kearney voted to move LB1 to the Revenue Committee, where Revisor of Statutes Marcia McClurg recommended it be referenced.\n\nBoth senators had previously voted to reference LB1 to the Government Committee, which has jurisdiction over matters affecting political subdivisions, because of how it affected city and county governments.\n\nBackers of Pillen's plan said the move was an attempt to kill LB1 or block it from advancing to the floor. At least half of the members of the Government Committee have voiced skepticism or opposition to Pillen's plan.\n\nOn Monday, Lowe said a letter from Linehan and Sen. Tom Brewer of Gordon, the chairman of the Government Committee, requesting LB1 be re-referenced swayed him to change his thinking.\n\n\"I always respect two committee chairs coming together,\" Lowe said.\n\nOpposing the re-referencing motion were Omaha Sen. Tony Vargas, Sen. Merv Riepe of Ralston and Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar.\n\nPillen's plan would implement a 5.5% sales tax on previously untaxed goods and services, a 4% tax on real property maintenance and repairs, a 2% tax on purchase and repair of agricultural machinery, and raise taxes on candy, soda, cigarettes and alcohol.\n\nIt also eliminates school districts' ability to levy property taxes and caps growth on property tax revenue for cities and counties.\n\nThe Revenue Committee is scheduled to hold a public hearing on the bill at 9:30 a.m. Tuesday.\n\nThe Referencing Committee on Monday also debated where to assign a bill (LB29) from Sen. Jen Day of Gretna requiring the sale of a state-owned plane valued at $3.6 million used by Pillen and members of the executive branch.\n\nUnder Day's plan, the funds from the sale would be returned to the state's general fund and put toward property tax relief efforts -- the stated purpose of the special session.\n\nThe plane made approximately 50 flights this summer, often staying in the air for less than 30 minutes. On July 25, the first day of the special session, the Beech C90GTx King Air flew three flights connecting Lincoln, Beatrice and Nebraska City.\n\nDay said the state should consider cutting non-essential spending before slashing $200 million from programs like those focused on foster children first.\n\n\"If property tax relief is as important to the governor as he says it is, I would hope we would look at luxuries before essentials,\" Day said.\n\nLincoln Sen. Eliot Bostar argued the Executive Board previously handled the task of deciding which plane the state would purchase more than a decade ago and that the question should remain with the nine-member committee now.\n\nThe motion to reference it to the Executive Board failed 3-6, however, with Bostar, Lincoln Sen. Beau Ballard and Sen. Mike Jacobson of North Platte voting in support.\n\nSenators then voted 8-1 to reference the bill to the Transportation and Telecommunications Committee, which has jurisdiction over the Nebraska Department of Transportation's aeronautics division.\n\nBostar cast the lone vote against the motion.\n\nTop Journal Star photos for July 2024\n\nReach the writer at 402-473-7120 or cdunker@journalstar.com.\n\nOn Twitter @ChrisDunkerLJS\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy. {"uri": "8251077124", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-30", "time": "17:21:01", "dateTime": "2024-07-30T17:21:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-30T17:20:21Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8117647171020508, "url": "https://nptelegraph.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/nebraska-business-leader-disputes-urgency-and-size-of-property-tax-problem/article_d65522ba-adfd-5faf-b0b5-3717b2557c8e.html", "title": "Nebraska business leader disputes urgency and size of property tax problem", "body": "LINCOLN -- A key business leader told state lawmakers Monday that Nebraska's property taxes are not as far out of whack as Gov. Jim Pillen contends.\n\nNebraska Chamber of Commerce President Bryan Slone made the argument during the first day of public hearings on bills introduced during the special legislative session that started Thursday. He testified on behalf of the state, Omaha and Lincoln Chambers of Commerce.\n\nWhile Slone said he is a \"very unhappy property taxpayer personally,\" he cited figures from the national Tax Foundation showing property taxes account for a similar share of state and local taxes paid in Nebraska as in neighboring states and urged lawmakers to spend more time studying the problem before taking what he called risky action.\n\n\"We don't have a $2 billion problem,\" he said, referring to the amount of property taxes that Pillen has targeted for cutting. \"There isn't a need to reduce property taxes 40% to be competitive.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... Nebraska's Ty Robinson, Lincoln company prove clever NIL deals do still exist As police kill Native Americans on this reservation, families left afraid and in the dark Chadron State student wins 'You Can Be A Star' at Lincoln County Fair Lincoln County Fair has the beef: Youngest of 4 siblings, Josie Wilson wows with 5 purple ribbons First-timer wins dairy goat reserve champion; competitors surprised with coach as judge Seward's time capsule -- filled with letters, booze and a car -- to be opened next year United States women's volleyball team loses Olympics opener to China 'Exhausting, heartbreaking, devastating': Racism at Wayne school pushes family out of Nebraska Nebraska Legislature reassigns Pillen's property tax plan to Revenue Committee Cash was 'some pig' for all the wrong reasons at Lincoln County Fair 7 killed after plane departing from Nebraska City crashes in Wyoming including members of gospel group Semi crash near Gothenburg results in no injuries 18-year-old Ogallala teen dies in wreck at dead end of A Street Thursday Drag queens help open Olympics but 'Last Supper' tableau draws criticism 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street\n\nBut Scott Peterson, a rancher and an attorney who lives near Kilgore, testified that there is a \"substantial difference\" between the property taxes he pays on land he owns in Nebraska and land he owns in South Dakota. He supported a proposal to ratchet down the maximum school property tax levy over the next decade.\n\n\"This is a path that makes sense,\" he said.\n\nThat plan, introduced by State Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, drew the most interest of the seven bills heard Monday. The seven addressed property taxes, the focus of the special session, in a variety of ways. They did not include Pillen's proposal, which is scheduled for a hearing Tuesday.\n\nThe governor's plan would create a new property tax credit worth $2.03 billion in the first year, aimed at offsetting school property taxes but distributed based on property valuation rather than the amount of school taxes paid by property owners. It would pay for the credit by increasing or imposing sales taxes and sin taxes on a number of goods and services, bringing in about $900 million, while capping property tax revenues collected by cities and counties.\n\nThe plan would leave major issues to be addressed by future lawmakers, including how to fulfill Pillen's goal of having state dollars replace property taxes for school operating costs. Legislative Bill 1, introduced on his behalf by Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of the Omaha area, would state the Legislature's intent to revamp the state school aid formula before the 2026-27 school year.\n\nAmong the seven bills heard Monday were measures setting a higher income tax rate for the wealthiest Nebraskans, creating a special tax on luxury items, raising cigarette taxes and offering income tax credits to renters who would not get direct benefit from property tax reductions.\n\nHughes' LB 9, which she called the \"Lower the Levy Cap\" bill, could offer an alternative to Pillen's plan. She said it would provide Nebraskans with stable, sustainable property tax relief, while preserving local control of schools.\n\nThe measure would trigger additional state aid for schools and force reductions in the amount of property taxes schools would be allowed to collect. She said the approach was better than the bill Pillen is pushing.\n\n\"I don't feel we're going to fix our way out of this problem with a tax credit,\" she said. \"The maximum levy cap for schools is the ultimate hard cap.\"\n\nUnder her plan, the school levy lid would drop from the current $1.05 down to 65 cents next year, then drop another 10 cents every two years until it reaches 25 cents in 2033. The lower lids would trigger additional state aid for schools and reduce the disparities in tax levies from district to district. It also would make changes in the state school aid formula to direct more aid to agricultural-based districts.\n\nLB 9 drew support from agricultural groups, including the Nebraska Farm Bureau and Nebraska Farmers Union, and from some education representatives. Other education leaders, including groups representing school boards, school administrators and teachers, took neutral positions, saying they wanted to see models of how the bill would affect individual school districts.\n\nSpencer Head, speaking for the Omaha Public Schools, objected to two provisions of the bill. One would eliminate the averaging adjustment, a factor in the state school aid formula that helps larger districts with lower than average per-school spending. The other would steer aid too much in the direction of rural districts.\n\nmartha.stoddard@owh.com, 402-670-2402, twitter.com/stoddardOWH\n\nGet Government & Politics updates in your inbox!\n\nStay {"uri": "8249511106", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "20:14:56", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T20:14:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T20:14:05Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7803921699523926, "url": "https://nptelegraph.com/nebraska-legislature-reassigns-pillens-property-tax-plan-to-revenue-committee/article_e7dc9cd9-a935-5a0a-8dd1-34010c0b56e3.html", "title": "Nebraska Legislature reassigns Pillen's property tax plan to Revenue Committee", "body": "Gov. Jim Pillen's plan to cut property taxes will go before the Revenue Committee later this week after lawmakers voted on Monday to change its committee assignment.\n\nThe Legislature's Referencing Committee voted 6-3 to move LB1 out of the Government, Military and Veterans Affairs Committee, where it was assigned in a surprise move last week, and into the Revenue Committee.\n\nIn the committee led by Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of Elkhorn, who introduced LB1 on Pillen's behalf on the first day of a special session called by the governor, the bill will likely have an easier path to the floor of the Legislature.\n\nThe move also gives any amendments proposed by the Revenue Committee priority when the bill comes up for debate by the full Legislature.\n\nPeople are also reading... Nebraska's Ty Robinson, Lincoln company prove clever NIL deals do still exist As police kill Native Americans on this reservation, families left afraid and in the dark Chadron State student wins 'You Can Be A Star' at Lincoln County Fair Lincoln County Fair has the beef: Youngest of 4 siblings, Josie Wilson wows with 5 purple ribbons First-timer wins dairy goat reserve champion; competitors surprised with coach as judge Seven veterans honored with Quilts of Valor at North Platte Pow Wow 18-year-old Ogallala teen dies in wreck at dead end of A Street Thursday Cash was 'some pig' for all the wrong reasons at Lincoln County Fair Semi crash near Gothenburg results in no injuries Drag queens help open Olympics but 'Last Supper' tableau draws criticism 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Seward's time capsule -- filled with letters, booze and a car -- to be opened next year North Platte man sentenced to 10 to 15 years for domestic assault 'Ready to learn from this': John Cook updates Harper Murray's status for 2024 season This Nebraska couple run a senior dog sanctuary\n\nSen. Ray Aguilar of Grand Island, the chairman of the Executive Board, which also serves as the Referencing Committee, and Sen. John Lowe of Kearney voted to move LB1 to the Revenue Committee, where Revisor of Statutes Marcia McClurg recommended it be referenced.\n\nBoth senators had previously voted to reference LB1 to the Government Committee, which has jurisdiction over matters affecting political subdivisions, because of how it affected city and county governments.\n\nBackers of Pillen's plan said the move was an attempt to kill LB1 or block it from advancing to the floor. At least half of the members of the Government Committee have voiced skepticism or opposition to Pillen's plan.\n\nOn Monday, Lowe said a letter from Linehan and Sen. Tom Brewer of Gordon, the chairman of the Government Committee, requesting LB1 be re-referenced swayed him to change his thinking.\n\n\"I always respect two committee chairs coming together,\" Lowe said.\n\nOpposing the re-referencing motion were Omaha Sen. Tony Vargas, Sen. Merv Riepe of Ralston and Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar.\n\nPillen's plan would implement a 5.5% sales tax on previously untaxed goods and services, a 4% tax on real property maintenance and repairs, a 2% tax on purchase and repair of agricultural machinery, and raise taxes on candy, soda, cigarettes and alcohol.\n\nIt also eliminates school districts' ability to levy property taxes and caps growth on property tax revenue for cities and counties.\n\nThe Revenue Committee is scheduled to hold a public hearing on the bill at 9:30 a.m. Tuesday.\n\nThe Referencing Committee on Monday also debated where to assign a bill (LB29) from Sen. Jen Day of Gretna requiring the sale of a state-owned plane valued at $3.6 million used by Pillen and members of the executive branch.\n\nUnder Day's plan, the funds from the sale would be returned to the state's general fund and put toward property tax relief efforts -- the stated purpose of the special session.\n\nThe plane made approximately 50 flights this summer, often staying in the air for less than 30 minutes. On July 25, the first day of the special session, the Beech C90GTx King Air flew three flights connecting Lincoln, Beatrice and Nebraska City.\n\nDay said the state should consider cutting non-essential spending before slashing $200 million from programs like those focused on foster children first.\n\n\"If property tax relief is as important to the governor as he says it is, I would hope we would look at luxuries before essentials,\" Day said.\n\nLincoln Sen. Eliot Bostar argued the Executive Board previously handled the task of deciding which plane the state would purchase more than a decade ago and that the question should remain with the nine-member committee now.\n\nThe motion to reference it to the Executive Board failed 3-6, however, with Bostar, Lincoln Sen. Beau Ballard and Sen. Mike Jacobson of North Platte voting in support.\n\nSenators then voted 8-1 to reference the bill to the Transportation and Telecommunications Committee, which has jurisdiction over the Nebraska Department of Transportation's aeronautics division.\n\nBostar cast the lone vote against the motion.\n\nReach the writer at 402-473-7120 or cdunker@journalstar.com.\n\nOn Twitter {"uri": "8251045652", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-30", "time": "16:57:12", "dateTime": "2024-07-30T16:57:12Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-30T16:56:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7529411911964417, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/nebraska-business-leader-disputes-urgency-and-size-of-property-tax-problem/article_935c5792-a4b8-5fea-8ad2-8b1660b5b1fe.html", "title": "Nebraska business leader disputes urgency and size of property tax problem", "body": "LINCOLN -- A key business leader told state lawmakers Monday that Nebraska's property taxes are not as far out of whack as Gov. Jim Pillen contends.\n\nNebraska Chamber of Commerce President Bryan Slone made the argument during the first day of public hearings on bills introduced during the special legislative session that started Thursday. He testified on behalf of the state, Omaha and Lincoln Chambers of Commerce.\n\nWhile Slone said he is a \"very unhappy property taxpayer personally,\" he cited figures from the national Tax Foundation showing property taxes account for a similar share of state and local taxes paid in Nebraska as in neighboring states and urged lawmakers to spend more time studying the problem before taking what he called risky action.\n\n\"We don't have a $2 billion problem,\" he said, referring to the amount of property taxes that Pillen has targeted for cutting. \"There isn't a need to reduce property taxes 40% to be competitive.\"\n\nPeople are also reading... Sierra signage going up at former Sioux City Bed Bath & Beyond North Sioux City crash kills one 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Now streaming and on DVD: 'The Richmond Way' gives us the whole 'Ted Lasso' story BNSF Railway wants to remove collapsed Big Sioux River train bridge Sioux City's Lyons Park getting $1.22 million in upgrades Jason's Frozen Treats open second dine-in ice cream parlor Sioux City driver charged with vehicular homicide from weekend crash Body found in Floyd River in Sioux City Elk Point man killed in North Sioux City crash Wife of Dakota Dunes fatal stabbing suspect charged as an accessory Financial situation for McCook Lake residents still murky a month after flood State baseball: Scrapy dynamic duo sends Crusaders to state title game, down top seed Wahlert Woodbury County Court Report for July 22, 2024 Two dead following Sunday night two-vehicle accident in Sioux City's westside\n\nBut Scott Peterson, a rancher and an attorney who lives near Kilgore, testified that there is a \"substantial difference\" between the property taxes he pays on land he owns in Nebraska and land he owns in South Dakota. He supported a proposal to ratchet down the maximum school property tax levy over the next decade.\n\n\"This is a path that makes sense,\" he said.\n\nThat plan, introduced by State Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, drew the most interest of the seven bills heard Monday. The seven addressed property taxes, the focus of the special session, in a variety of ways. They did not include Pillen's proposal, which is scheduled for a hearing Tuesday.\n\nThe governor's plan would create a new property tax credit worth $2.03 billion in the first year, aimed at offsetting school property taxes but distributed based on property valuation rather than the amount of school taxes paid by property owners. It would pay for the credit by increasing or imposing sales taxes and sin taxes on a number of goods and services, bringing in about $900 million, while capping property tax revenues collected by cities and counties.\n\nThe plan would leave major issues to be addressed by future lawmakers, including how to fulfill Pillen's goal of having state dollars replace property taxes for school operating costs. Legislative Bill 1, introduced on his behalf by Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of the Omaha area, would state the Legislature's intent to revamp the state school aid formula before the 2026-27 school year.\n\nAmong the seven bills heard Monday were measures setting a higher income tax rate for the wealthiest Nebraskans, creating a special tax on luxury items, raising cigarette taxes and offering income tax credits to renters who would not get direct benefit from property tax reductions.\n\nHughes' LB 9, which she called the \"Lower the Levy Cap\" bill, could offer an alternative to Pillen's plan. She said it would provide Nebraskans with stable, sustainable property tax relief, while preserving local control of schools.\n\nThe measure would trigger additional state aid for schools and force reductions in the amount of property taxes schools would be allowed to collect. She said the approach was better than the bill Pillen is pushing.\n\n\"I don't feel we're going to fix our way out of this problem with a tax credit,\" she said. \"The maximum levy cap for schools is the ultimate hard cap.\"\n\nUnder her plan, the school levy lid would drop from the current $1.05 down to 65 cents next year, then drop another 10 cents every two years until it reaches 25 cents in 2033. The lower lids would trigger additional state aid for schools and reduce the disparities in tax levies from district to district. It also would make changes in the state school aid formula to direct more aid to agricultural-based districts.\n\nLB 9 drew support from agricultural groups, including the Nebraska Farm Bureau and Nebraska Farmers Union, and from some education representatives. Other education leaders, including groups representing school boards, school administrators and teachers, took neutral positions, saying they wanted to see models of how the bill would affect individual school districts.\n\nSpencer Head, speaking for the Omaha Public Schools, objected to two provisions of the bill. One would eliminate the averaging adjustment, a factor in the state school aid formula that helps larger districts with lower than average per-school spending. The other would steer aid too much in the direction of rural districts.\n\nmartha.stoddard@owh.com, 402-670-2402, twitter.com/stoddardOWH\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nGet {"uri": "8249682158", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "23:31:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T23:31:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T23:29:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7529411911964417, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/nebraska-lawmakers-alternative-tax-plan-gets-warm-reception-at-public-hearing/article_70cd2608-f322-5d21-9ff9-60388159a2ff.html", "title": "Nebraska lawmaker's 'alternative' tax plan gets warm reception at public hearing", "body": "Nebraska might legalize online sports betting to address a property tax crisis. A legislative session has begun to introduce and discuss a relevant bill.\n\nA proposal that has emerged as a potential alternative to Nebraska Gov. Jim Pillen's controversial property tax relief plan won cautious support from several key interest groups at a public hearing Monday, which marked Day 3 of the special legislative session Pillen called to take up property tax reform.\n\nDrafted by a bipartisan group of lawmakers and introduced by Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, the bill (LB9) would lower the maximum property tax rate local school districts could levy by 80 cents over the course of a decade -- a 76% cut to the primary driver of high property tax bills in Nebraska.\n\nThe proposal calls for the maximum levy amount per $100 of taxable valuation of property to drop from $1.05 now to 65 cents next year before gradually reducing to 25 cents by 2034.\n\nPeople are also reading... Woodbury County Court Report for July 22, 2024 BNSF Railway wants to remove collapsed Big Sioux River train bridge North Sioux City crash kills one Jason's Frozen Treats open second dine-in ice cream parlor 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Now streaming and on DVD: 'The Richmond Way' gives us the whole 'Ted Lasso' story Two dead following Sunday night two-vehicle accident in Sioux City's westside Sioux City's Lyons Park getting $1.22 million in upgrades Sioux City driver charged with vehicular homicide from weekend crash Body found in Floyd River in Sioux City Elk Point man killed in North Sioux City crash Wife of Dakota Dunes fatal stabbing suspect charged as an accessory State baseball: Scrapy dynamic duo sends Crusaders to state title game, down top seed Wahlert Financial situation for McCook Lake residents still murky a month after flood Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for July 23, 2024\n\nSuch a move would require the state to increase its aid to public schools by about $1 billion next year and another $253 million every two years after that, Hughes told the Legislature's Revenue Committee at Monday's hearing.\n\nThe bill, like Pillen's preferred plan, calls for the front-loading of existing property tax credits to cover about $560 million of the initial $1 billion necessary to fund the property tax cuts. But the legislation leaves crucial questions over how to fund the remaining $440 million balance unanswered.\n\nThat's on purpose, Hughes told the committee Monday.\n\n\"I didn't want to kill the bill by putting in a revenue source,\" she said.\n\nInstead, she hopes Nebraska's single-house Legislature can come to a consensus on how to fund the balance of the plan -- a task that has proved difficult this year at the Capitol, where lawmakers in April sank Pillen's initial tax plan over its reliance on the elimination of numerous sales tax exemptions.\n\nThe governor has doubled down on his plan, proposing new legislation this month that relies on the same sales tax increases that have drawn steady opposition from various industry groups and lawmakers whose support he would need for his bill to become law.\n\nBut Hughes' plan -- which she drafted alongside Sens. Tom Brandt of Plymouth, Danielle Conrad of Lincoln, Myron Dorn of Adams and Lynn Walz of Fremont, all of whom are co-sponsors of the legislation -- has so far avoided the opposition that has plagued Pillen's plan.\n\nAt Monday's hearing, lawmakers who in April championed Pillen's proposal praised the concept offered by Hughes.\n\nSen. Lou Ann Linehan of Elkhorn, the Revenue Committee chairwoman who introduced Pillen's plan on the governor's behalf last week, called LB9 \"impressive.\" Sen. Fred Meyer of St. Paul, a Pillen appointee, told Hughes: \"Your plan is a good plan.\"\n\nAnd key groups that have signaled public opposition to the governor's plan -- including the Nebraska Farmers Union and Nebraska Farm Bureau -- testified as proponents of the Hughes' proposal or offered cautious support while testifying in a neutral capacity.\n\nBruce Rieker, the top lobbyist for the Farm Bureau who testified in support of LB9 on behalf of that group and eight other agricultural groups in the state, said the plan \"contains several components that address our state's overreliance on property taxes\" and praised its incremental approach.\n\n\"We want this to be right, even if it doesn't mean right now,\" Rieker told lawmakers. \"And we would rather see you put the state on a trajectory that makes this meaningful and sustainable, and that you do it in a thoughtful and deliberate way.\"\n\nAmong others who testified in support of LB9 at Monday's hearing were representatives from the Nebraska Rural Community Schools Association, Schools Taking Action for Nebraska Childrens' Education and several school districts across the state, including Centennial Public School, which serves about 500 students in Utica.\n\nJason Richters, a farmer and the Centennial Public School Board president, praised the bill's maintenance of local control of public schools, which has emerged as a chief concern hanging over Pillen's plan to cut property taxes by putting nearly all public school funding in the hands of the state.\n\n\"Nebraskans are passionate about maintaining local control of their schools and the education offered to their children,\" Richters said. \"I believe they're unwilling to jeopardize that for a few more dollars in tax relief.\"\n\nRepresentatives from the Nebraska State Education Association, the Nebraska Association of County Officials and the state Association of School Boards praised Hughes' plan but withheld full endorsements as they wait for further details to be ironed out.\n\n\"There's a lot here to support,\" said Colby Coash, a lobbyist for the school board group and the Nebraska Council of School Administrators.\n\nWhile groups from the public sector broadly voiced support of the plan, business groups lined up in opposition.\n\nBryan Slone, the president of the Nebraska Chamber of Commerce, said the group's \"biggest issue\" with LB9 is that it fails to identify the scale of the problem lawmakers were recalled to Lincoln to fix.\n\n\"Which is: How big is our property tax problem?\" Slone said. \"Do we need to reduce property taxes by 10 or 20 or 30 or 40 or 50 percent to be competitive with other states? It would be nice to define that before we start raising the (sales and/or income) taxes.\"\n\nIncome, property and sales taxes make up the \"three-legged stool\" that defines state revenues, which have grown increasingly reliant on property taxes as lawmakers have passed corporate and individual income tax cuts in recent years.\n\nIn the fiscal year that ended June 30, the state's total net tax revenues included $3.6 billion in sales taxes, $4.2 million in income taxes and $5.1 billion in property taxes.\n\nIncome tax revenues are projected to drop by $1 billion by 2027 as a result of prior tax cuts, but Pillen has made \"transformational\" property tax cuts the top priority of his second year as Nebraska's governor.\n\n\"I'm not saying there isn't a property tax problem,\" Slone said Monday. \"But when we say we need to reduce property taxes by 40 or 50 percent to be competitive, that's just not true.\"\n\nThe Lincoln Independent Business Association and the conservative fiscal group The Platte Institute also opposed Hughes' plan Monday.\n\nAnd the otherwise warm reception LB9 received at Monday's hearing could sour when new revenue streams become part of the legislation.\n\nIn an exchange with Sen. Eliot Bostar of Lincoln, Rieker, the Farm Bureau's lobbyist, acknowledged that the group would no longer support the plan if the bill included new sales taxes on agricultural inputs or equipment to fund property tax relief.\n\n\"That would be a career-ending move on my part,\" Rieker joked.\n\nThe exchange highlights the challenge lawmakers will face in trying to identify new revenue streams without angering influential industry groups and threatening the popularity of any property tax relief proposal.\n\nBut lawmakers will have numerous revenue-generating proposals to consider when they begin deliberations next week, including new ideas put forward Monday, which marked the last day senators could introduce legislation to be considered during the special legislative session.\n\nLawmakers have introduced 81 bills and 24 constitutional amendments to be considered this session, including 64 pieces of legislation put forward Monday. Among the latest batch of proposals:\n\nMARIJUANA LEGALIZATION: Two Omaha senators proposed legalizing and taxing marijuana for recreational use.\n\nSen. Justin Wayne proposed (LB52) setting a 25% excise tax on cultivators who sell or transfer unprocessed cannabis to manufacturers or stores, as well as a 25% sales tax to be paid by consumers.\n\nThe proceeds from Wayne's bill, after expenses to administer the Cannabis Control Act, would be directed into the Education Future Fund which provides an equal amount of funding for all public school students in Nebraska.\n\nSen. Terrell McKinney proposed (LB71) a 16% excise and sales tax on cannabis products in Nebraska.\n\nUnder McKinney's bill, 50% of the proceeds from the sales tax would be transferred to the property tax credit cash fund and 40% would go to the Education Future Fund used to support public schools. The remainder would be used for grants tied to business development, prisoner reentry, education and youth outreach.\n\nMANSION TAX: Sen. Carol Blood of Bellevue sponsored a bill (LB36) implementing a 1.25% real estate transfer tax on all single-family homes valued at more than $800,000.\n\nLUXURY TAXES: Nebraskans would pay more in taxes on a slew of luxury items under a bill (LB54) from Wayne. Among the luxury items taxed at 2.25% included in the legislation are vehicles over $50,000; jewelry that costs more than $5,000; clothing that costs more than $1,000.\n\nMore expensive vehicles, watercraft, aircraft, and jewelry and clothing would also be subject to higher tax rates, under Wayne's plan.\n\nCAPITAL GAINS: Unrealized capital gains for individuals, corporations, or others would be taxed at 10% beginning in 2025, under a bill (LB51) from Wayne, with that amount declining to 8% in 2026 and 7% in 2027.\n\nEXCISE TAX ON AMMO: Wayne also introduced a bill (LB65) that would add a 5% excise tax on all purchases of ammunition in Nebraska.\n\nHalf of the revenue generated would go to the Property Tax Credit Cash Fund, while 35% would go to the Wildlife Management Grant Program to be run by the Nebraska Game and Parks Commission, and 15% to the Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder Grant Program Fund.\n\nTEEOSA SUNSET: Sen. Steve Halloran of Hastings, along with 13 co-sponsors, brought a bill (LB81) that would sunset the Tax Equity and Educational Opportunities Support Act, better known as TEEOSA.\n\nEnacted in 1990, TEEOSA calculates the difference between what schools need to educate every child from what resources are available in property taxes to determine how much state aid a district should receive.\n\nHalloran, who will be term-limited this year, said the sunset included in LB81 would end TEEOSA in 2027 without identifying a replacement, requiring the next Legislature to figure out a solution.\n\nTAX LAWSUITS: Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar introduced several proposed constitutional amendments granting individuals the right to sue political subdivisions for levying new taxes (LR7CA), increasing tax rates (LR8CA), extending expiring taxes (LR9CA), changing policies that result in a net tax revenue gain (LR10CA), or enter into financial obligations without pledged cash reserves (LR11CA) that have not been approved by voters at a November general election.\n\nSlama also introduced constitutional amendments that would require political subdivisions to receive voter approval to levy new taxes (LR12CA), increase tax rates (LR13CA), extend expiring taxes (LR14CA), and change policies that result in a new tax revenue gain (LR15CA), and enter into financial obligations without pledged cash reserves (LR16CA).\n\nDIFFERENT TAX METHOD: Omaha Sen. Mike McDonnell's proposed constitutional amendment (LR24CA) would allow the Legislature to create a separate method for taxing residential properties, such as limiting the growth of valuations.\n\nPhotos: Nebraska Legislature gavels in for first day of 2024 session\n\nReach the writer at 402-473-7223 or awegley@journalstar.com. {"uri": "2024-07-436776753", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "07:48:21", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T07:48:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T07:16:59Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7411764860153198, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/legislative-maneuvering-could-complicate-path-for-pillens-tax-plan/article_46d8fc3f-4ffa-58d3-affd-ef35e7c5e7bb.html", "title": "Legislative maneuvering could complicate path for Pillen's tax plan", "body": "ZACH WENDLING\n\nNebraska Examiner\n\nLINCOLN -- Some lawmakers sent a warning to Nebraska Gov. Jim Pillen on Friday by voting to send the core of his property tax relief proposal to a more unfavorable committee than anticipated.\n\nThe Revenue Committee is usually the destination for bills dealing with taxation and property taxes. All introduced bills and policy resolutions must get a public hearing and must be sent to the \"appropriate committee,\" per the Legislature's internal rules.\n\nWhen it came to Legislative Bill 1, introduced by State Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of Elkhorn on Pillen's behalf, the internally selected, 10-member Executive Board took a different approach. Five of the nine voting members determined the best destination for the 139-page proposal would be the Government, Military and Veterans Affairs Committee.\n\nTheir reasoning: It touches various aspects of government overall, not just taxes.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nWoodbury County Court Report for July 22, 2024 BNSF Railway wants to remove collapsed Big Sioux River train bridge North Sioux City crash kills one Jason's Frozen Treats open second dine-in ice cream parlor 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Two dead following Sunday night two-vehicle accident in Sioux City's westside Now streaming and on DVD: 'The Richmond Way' gives us the whole 'Ted Lasso' story Sioux City's Lyons Park getting $1.22 million in upgrades Sioux City driver charged with vehicular homicide from weekend crash Wife of Dakota Dunes fatal stabbing suspect charged as an accessory State baseball: Scrapy dynamic duo sends Crusaders to state title game, down top seed Wahlert Marriage licenses for Woodbury County for July 23, 2024 82-year-old man gets 32-year prison sentence for child porn Financial situation for McCook Lake residents still murky a month after flood 2 sentenced to prison for selling fentanyl in Sioux City\n\n\"Logically, it could have been sent to 10 different committees,\" State Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar, an Executive Board member, told the Nebraska Examiner. \"The key for me was that the overarching theme is the authority of government.\"\n\nPossibly less friendly committee\n\nDuring debate on a previous Pillen-led tax package this spring, the Government Committee chair, State Sen. Tom Brewer of north-central Nebraska, was the only committee member who voted for the bill. Seven other members opposed or otherwise did not vote for the bill.\n\nState Sens. Ray Aguilar of Grand Island and John Lowe of Kearney are members of both the Government Committee and the Executive Board, where they serve as chair and vice chair.\n\nThe committee shift is likely to be short-lived as Aguilar and Lowe both said they'd reverse their votes come Monday. Still, it sends a strong message to the first term governor, who already faces an uphill climb to get enough votes for the property tax relief ideas he's worked on all summer.\n\nIn addition to Aguilar and Lowe, State Sens. Merv Riepe of Ralston, Tony Vargas of Omaha and Slama agreed to the Government Committee pick. State Sens. John Arch of La Vista, Beau Ballard of Lincoln, Eliot Bostar of Lincoln and Mike Jacobson of North Platte voted against.\n\n'This is about taxes'\n\nArch, the Legislature's speaker, who sets the daily agenda, noted that there would likely be a committee amendment before LB 1, or any other major tax proposal this special session, makes it to the floor.\n\nHe encouraged his colleagues to consider which committee would be the most qualified to craft that compromise to deal with Nebraska's tax issue.\n\nLinehan joined Brewer on Friday in requesting that the Executive Board's vote be reconsidered.\n\n\"The subject matter of this bill -- pertaining to the assessment and collection of taxes -- is best heard in the Revenue Committee,\" Brewer and Linehan wrote in a joint letter.\n\nLinehan has the option to force a floor vote to send the bill to her committee if the Executive Board members do not reconsider. She said she is \"not the least concerned.\"\n\n\"Everybody in the whole state knows this is about taxes,\" Linehan told reporters, referring to LB 1.\n\n\"I don't think Nebraskans would be very happy when they find out that a handful of senators have decided that we should go home, enjoy the rest of our summer and not worry about the crisis of property taxes in the State of Nebraska.\"\n\nLowe, asked how he would vote Monday, said he has \"always honored the chairmen.\"\n\nSlama said even if an introducer has a preferred committee, she and the top-ranking board must \"look at the text of the bill and not the personalities involved.\"\n\nAnother bucks committee suggestion\n\nAnother bill had been suggested, based on its content, to be sent to the Education Committee but ultimately found a home in the Revenue Committee: LB 9, from State Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward.\n\nHughes' bill, the bipartisan product of work with four other lawmakers, is an alternative K-12 school funding model to reduce general fund property tax rates for districts over 10 years. It also addresses the main state aid funding formula for the state's 244 school districts.\n\nLawmakers voted 5-3 to send the bill to the Revenue Committee, rather than to the Education Committee.\n\nLB 9 is one of the first bills up for legislative hearings this Monday, scheduled for 9:30 a.m. That will also be the final day of bill introductions for the special session. As of Friday, 35 bills and six constitutional amendments had been introduced so far. The Legislature will not meet Saturday as initially scheduled.\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your {"uri": "2024-07-438119003", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-30", "time": "10:11:37", "dateTime": "2024-07-30T10:11:37Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-30T09:27:07Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7411764860153198, "url": "https://omaha.com/eedition/sunrise/articles/urgency-of-property-tax-problem-disputed/article_cd639c9b-ba79-5e02-85b6-15b72b523f57.html", "title": "Urgency of property tax problem disputed", "body": "LINCOLN -- A key business leader told state lawmakers Monday that Nebraska's property taxes are not as far out of whack as Gov. Jim Pillen contends.\n\nNebraska Chamber of Commerce President Bryan Slone made the argument during the first day of public hearings on bills introduced during the special legislative session that started Thursday. He testified on behalf of the state, Omaha and Lincoln Chambers of Commerce.\n\nWhile Slone said he is a \"very unhappy property taxpayer personally,\" he cited figures from the national Tax Foundation showing property taxes account for a similar share of state and local taxes paid in Nebraska as in neighboring states and urged lawmakers to spend more time studying the problem before taking what he called risky action.\n\n\"We don't have a $2 billion problem,\" he said, referring to the amount of property taxes that Pillen has targeted for cutting. \"There isn't a need to reduce property taxes 40% to be competitive.\"\n\nBut Scott Peterson, a rancher and an attorney who lives near Kilgore, testified that there is a \"substantial difference\" between the property taxes he pays on land he owns in Nebraska and land he owns in South Dakota. He supported a proposal to ratchet down the maximum school property tax levy over the next decade.\n\n\"This is a path that makes sense,\" he said.\n\nThat plan, introduced by State Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, drew the most interest of the seven bills heard Monday. The seven addressed property taxes, the focus of the special session, in a variety of ways. They did not include Pillen's proposal, which is scheduled for a hearing Tuesday.\n\nThe governor's plan would create a new property tax credit worth $2.03 billion in the first year, aimed at offsetting school property taxes but distributed based on property valuation rather than the amount of school taxes paid by property owners. It would pay for the credit by increasing or imposing sales taxes and sin taxes on a number of goods and services, bringing in about $900 million, while capping property tax revenues collected by cities and counties.\n\nThe plan would leave major issues to be addressed by future lawmakers, including how to fulfill Pillen's goal of having state dollars replace property taxes for school operating costs. Legislative Bill 1, introduced on his behalf by Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of the Omaha area, would state the Legislature's intent to revamp the state school aid formula before the 2026-27 school year.\n\nAmong the seven bills heard Monday were measures setting a higher income tax rate for the wealthiest Nebraskans, creating a special tax on luxury items, raising cigarette taxes and offering income tax credits to renters who would not get direct benefit from property tax reductions.\n\nHughes' LB 9, which she called the \"Lower the Levy Cap\" bill, could offer an alternative to Pillen's plan. She said it would provide Nebraskans with stable, sustainable property tax relief, while preserving local control of schools.\n\nThe measure would trigger additional state aid for schools and force reductions in the amount of property taxes schools would be allowed to collect. She said the approach was better than the bill Pillen is pushing.\n\n\"I don't feel we're going to fix our way out of this problem with a tax credit,\" she said. \"The maximum levy cap for schools is the ultimate hard cap.\"\n\nUnder her plan, the school levy lid would drop from the current $1.05 down to 65 cents next year, then drop another 10 cents every two years until it reaches 25 cents in 2033. The lower lids would trigger additional state aid for schools and reduce the disparities in tax levies from district to district. It also would make changes in the state school aid formula to direct more aid to agricultural-based districts.\n\nLB 9 drew support from agricultural groups, including the Nebraska Farm Bureau and Nebraska Farmers Union, and from some education representatives. Other education leaders, including groups representing school boards, school administrators and teachers, took neutral positions, saying they wanted to see models of how the bill would affect individual school districts.\n\nSpencer Head, speaking for the Omaha Public Schools, objected to two provisions of the bill. One would eliminate the averaging adjustment, a factor in the state school aid formula that helps larger districts with lower than average per-school spending. {"uri": "8249682361", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "23:30:36", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T23:30:36Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T23:29:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7176470756530762, "url": "https://nptelegraph.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/nebraska-lawmakers-alternative-tax-plan-gets-warm-reception-at-public-hearing/article_be880fed-6f47-5f70-a485-51b909ab930f.html", "title": "Nebraska lawmaker's 'alternative' tax plan gets warm reception at public hearing", "body": "Nebraska might legalize online sports betting to address a property tax crisis. A legislative session has begun to introduce and discuss a relevant bill.\n\nA proposal that has emerged as a potential alternative to Nebraska Gov. Jim Pillen's controversial property tax relief plan won cautious support from several key interest groups at a public hearing Monday, which marked Day 3 of the special legislative session Pillen called to take up property tax reform.\n\nDrafted by a bipartisan group of lawmakers and introduced by Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, the bill (LB9) would lower the maximum property tax rate local school districts could levy by 80 cents over the course of a decade -- a 76% cut to the primary driver of high property tax bills in Nebraska.\n\nThe proposal calls for the maximum levy amount per $100 of taxable valuation of property to drop from $1.05 now to 65 cents next year before gradually reducing to 25 cents by 2034.\n\nPeople are also reading... Nebraska's Ty Robinson, Lincoln company prove clever NIL deals do still exist As police kill Native Americans on this reservation, families left afraid and in the dark Chadron State student wins 'You Can Be A Star' at Lincoln County Fair Lincoln County Fair has the beef: Youngest of 4 siblings, Josie Wilson wows with 5 purple ribbons First-timer wins dairy goat reserve champion; competitors surprised with coach as judge Seven veterans honored with Quilts of Valor at North Platte Pow Wow 18-year-old Ogallala teen dies in wreck at dead end of A Street Thursday Seward's time capsule -- filled with letters, booze and a car -- to be opened next year Cash was 'some pig' for all the wrong reasons at Lincoln County Fair Semi crash near Gothenburg results in no injuries Drag queens help open Olympics but 'Last Supper' tableau draws criticism 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street North Platte man sentenced to 10 to 15 years for domestic assault 'Ready to learn from this': John Cook updates Harper Murray's status for 2024 season 'Exhausting, heartbreaking, devastating': Racism at Wayne school pushes family out of Nebraska\n\nSuch a move would require the state to increase its aid to public schools by about $1 billion next year and another $253 million every two years after that, Hughes told the Legislature's Revenue Committee at Monday's hearing.\n\nThe bill, like Pillen's preferred plan, calls for the front-loading of existing property tax credits to cover about $560 million of the initial $1 billion necessary to fund the property tax cuts. But the legislation leaves crucial questions over how to fund the remaining $440 million balance unanswered.\n\nThat's on purpose, Hughes told the committee Monday.\n\n\"I didn't want to kill the bill by putting in a revenue source,\" she said.\n\nInstead, she hopes Nebraska's single-house Legislature can come to a consensus on how to fund the balance of the plan -- a task that has proved difficult this year at the Capitol, where lawmakers in April sank Pillen's initial tax plan over its reliance on the elimination of numerous sales tax exemptions.\n\nThe governor has doubled down on his plan, proposing new legislation this month that relies on the same sales tax increases that have drawn steady opposition from various industry groups and lawmakers whose support he would need for his bill to become law.\n\nBut Hughes' plan -- which she drafted alongside Sens. Tom Brandt of Plymouth, Danielle Conrad of Lincoln, Myron Dorn of Adams and Lynn Walz of Fremont, all of whom are co-sponsors of the legislation -- has so far avoided the opposition that has plagued Pillen's plan.\n\nAt Monday's hearing, lawmakers who in April championed Pillen's proposal praised the concept offered by Hughes.\n\nSen. Lou Ann Linehan of Elkhorn, the Revenue Committee chairwoman who introduced Pillen's plan on the governor's behalf last week, called LB9 \"impressive.\" Sen. Fred Meyer of St. Paul, a Pillen appointee, told Hughes: \"Your plan is a good plan.\"\n\nAnd key groups that have signaled public opposition to the governor's plan -- including the Nebraska Farmers Union and Nebraska Farm Bureau -- testified as proponents of the Hughes' proposal or offered cautious support while testifying in a neutral capacity.\n\nBruce Rieker, the top lobbyist for the Farm Bureau who testified in support of LB9 on behalf of that group and eight other agricultural groups in the state, said the plan \"contains several components that address our state's overreliance on property taxes\" and praised its incremental approach.\n\n\"We want this to be right, even if it doesn't mean right now,\" Rieker told lawmakers. \"And we would rather see you put the state on a trajectory that makes this meaningful and sustainable, and that you do it in a thoughtful and deliberate way.\"\n\nAmong others who testified in support of LB9 at Monday's hearing were representatives from the Nebraska Rural Community Schools Association, Schools Taking Action for Nebraska Childrens' Education and several school districts across the state, including Centennial Public School, which serves about 500 students in Utica.\n\nJason Richters, a farmer and the Centennial Public School Board president, praised the bill's maintenance of local control of public schools, which has emerged as a chief concern hanging over Pillen's plan to cut property taxes by putting nearly all public school funding in the hands of the state.\n\n\"Nebraskans are passionate about maintaining local control of their schools and the education offered to their children,\" Richters said. \"I believe they're unwilling to jeopardize that for a few more dollars in tax relief.\"\n\nRepresentatives from the Nebraska State Education Association, the Nebraska Association of County Officials and the state Association of School Boards praised Hughes' plan but withheld full endorsements as they wait for further details to be ironed out.\n\n\"There's a lot here to support,\" said Colby Coash, a lobbyist for the school board group and the Nebraska Council of School Administrators.\n\nWhile groups from the public sector broadly voiced support of the plan, business groups lined up in opposition.\n\nBryan Slone, the president of the Nebraska Chamber of Commerce, said the group's \"biggest issue\" with LB9 is that it fails to identify the scale of the problem lawmakers were recalled to Lincoln to fix.\n\n\"Which is: How big is our property tax problem?\" Slone said. \"Do we need to reduce property taxes by 10 or 20 or 30 or 40 or 50 percent to be competitive with other states? It would be nice to define that before we start raising the (sales and/or income) taxes.\"\n\nIncome, property and sales taxes make up the \"three-legged stool\" that defines state revenues, which have grown increasingly reliant on property taxes as lawmakers have passed corporate and individual income tax cuts in recent years.\n\nIn the fiscal year that ended June 30, the state's total net tax revenues included $3.6 billion in sales taxes, $4.2 million in income taxes and $5.1 billion in property taxes.\n\nIncome tax revenues are projected to drop by $1 billion by 2027 as a result of prior tax cuts, but Pillen has made \"transformational\" property tax cuts the top priority of his second year as Nebraska's governor.\n\n\"I'm not saying there isn't a property tax problem,\" Slone said Monday. \"But when we say we need to reduce property taxes by 40 or 50 percent to be competitive, that's just not true.\"\n\nThe Lincoln Independent Business Association and the conservative fiscal group The Platte Institute also opposed Hughes' plan Monday.\n\nAnd the otherwise warm reception LB9 received at Monday's hearing could sour when new revenue streams become part of the legislation.\n\nIn an exchange with Sen. Eliot Bostar of Lincoln, Rieker, the Farm Bureau's lobbyist, acknowledged that the group would no longer support the plan if the bill included new sales taxes on agricultural inputs or equipment to fund property tax relief.\n\n\"That would be a career-ending move on my part,\" Rieker joked.\n\nThe exchange highlights the challenge lawmakers will face in trying to identify new revenue streams without angering influential industry groups and threatening the popularity of any property tax relief proposal.\n\nBut lawmakers will have numerous revenue-generating proposals to consider when they begin deliberations next week, including new ideas put forward Monday, which marked the last day senators could introduce legislation to be considered during the special legislative session.\n\nLawmakers have introduced 81 bills and 24 constitutional amendments to be considered this session, including 64 pieces of legislation put forward Monday. Among the latest batch of proposals:\n\nMARIJUANA LEGALIZATION: Two Omaha senators proposed legalizing and taxing marijuana for recreational use.\n\nSen. Justin Wayne proposed (LB52) setting a 25% excise tax on cultivators who sell or transfer unprocessed cannabis to manufacturers or stores, as well as a 25% sales tax to be paid by consumers.\n\nThe proceeds from Wayne's bill, after expenses to administer the Cannabis Control Act, would be directed into the Education Future Fund which provides an equal amount of funding for all public school students in Nebraska.\n\nSen. Terrell McKinney proposed (LB71) a 16% excise and sales tax on cannabis products in Nebraska.\n\nUnder McKinney's bill, 50% of the proceeds from the sales tax would be transferred to the property tax credit cash fund and 40% would go to the Education Future Fund used to support public schools. The remainder would be used for grants tied to business development, prisoner reentry, education and youth outreach.\n\nMANSION TAX: Sen. Carol Blood of Bellevue sponsored a bill (LB36) implementing a 1.25% real estate transfer tax on all single-family homes valued at more than $800,000.\n\nLUXURY TAXES: Nebraskans would pay more in taxes on a slew of luxury items under a bill (LB54) from Wayne. Among the luxury items taxed at 2.25% included in the legislation are vehicles over $50,000; jewelry that costs more than $5,000; clothing that costs more than $1,000.\n\nMore expensive vehicles, watercraft, aircraft, and jewelry and clothing would also be subject to higher tax rates, under Wayne's plan.\n\nCAPITAL GAINS: Unrealized capital gains for individuals, corporations, or others would be taxed at 10% beginning in 2025, under a bill (LB51) from Wayne, with that amount declining to 8% in 2026 and 7% in 2027.\n\nEXCISE TAX ON AMMO: Wayne also introduced a bill (LB65) that would add a 5% excise tax on all purchases of ammunition in Nebraska.\n\nHalf of the revenue generated would go to the Property Tax Credit Cash Fund, while 35% would go to the Wildlife Management Grant Program to be run by the Nebraska Game and Parks Commission, and 15% to the Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder Grant Program Fund.\n\nTEEOSA SUNSET: Sen. Steve Halloran of Hastings, along with 13 co-sponsors, brought a bill (LB81) that would sunset the Tax Equity and Educational Opportunities Support Act, better known as TEEOSA.\n\nEnacted in 1990, TEEOSA calculates the difference between what schools need to educate every child from what resources are available in property taxes to determine how much state aid a district should receive.\n\nHalloran, who will be term-limited this year, said the sunset included in LB81 would end TEEOSA in 2027 without identifying a replacement, requiring the next Legislature to figure out a solution.\n\nTAX LAWSUITS: Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar introduced several proposed constitutional amendments granting individuals the right to sue political subdivisions for levying new taxes (LR7CA), increasing tax rates (LR8CA), extending expiring taxes (LR9CA), changing policies that result in a net tax revenue gain (LR10CA), or enter into financial obligations without pledged cash reserves (LR11CA) that have not been approved by voters at a November general election.\n\nSlama also introduced constitutional amendments that would require political subdivisions to receive voter approval to levy new taxes (LR12CA), increase tax rates (LR13CA), extend expiring taxes (LR14CA), and change policies that result in a new tax revenue gain (LR15CA), and enter into financial obligations without pledged cash reserves (LR16CA).\n\nDIFFERENT TAX METHOD: Omaha Sen. Mike McDonnell's proposed constitutional amendment (LR24CA) would allow the Legislature to create a separate method for taxing residential properties, such as limiting the growth of valuations.\n\nPhotos: Nebraska Legislature gavels in for first day of 2024 session\n\nReach the writer at 402-473-7223 or awegley@journalstar.com. Pillen's property tax relief plan up to $139 million short", "body": "Gov. Jim Pillen officially unviles his property tax plan as a summer-long attempt to find a path to reduce local property taxes. July 18, 2024. (Zach Wendling/Nebraska Examiner)\n\nLINCOLN -- As introduced, Gov. Jim Pillen's core property tax relief proposal could fall short by $139 million in annual estimated revenue needed.\n\nPillen's main proposal, introduced through Legislative Bill 1 in the special session, includes placing a sales tax on more than 100 currently tax-exempt goods and services. It would also raise seven \"sin\" taxes, on candy and soft drinks, vapes, cigarettes, keno gambling, spirits, consumable hemp and games of skill.\n\nIn total, the new or increased taxes would raise an estimated $1.15 billion in the first full fiscal year on state tax rolls. If passed, Pillen's office is targeting an Oct. 1 effective date, which is not yet included in LB 1.\n\nThose values rely upon estimates provided to lawmakers last Wednesday, one day before State Sen. Lou Ann Linehan introduced LB 1 on Pillen's behalf.\n\nHowever, the tax rates as introduced varied from the ones provided to reporters and lawmakers in the days leading up to the session, which a spokesperson said was likely the result of bill drafting errors.\n\nFor example, four currently exempt goods and services were suggested to be taxed at higher rates than were ultimately included in the bill:\n\nThe statewide sales tax rate is 5.5 cents per dollar purchase, plus any municipal or county sales tax rates, which range from an additional 0.5 cents to 2 cents.\n\nAccording to an analysis from the Nebraska Examiner, the Pillen-led proposal would fall short of his revenue goal by $139 million, largely because the projected machinery and equipment revenue was half of what was projected earlier.\n\nThat would complicate the governor's main tax relief pursuit: the state taking on 80% of local K-12 funding paid for through property taxes.\n\nA spokesperson for Pillen said the intention is to tax machinery and equipment at 4 cents and the change \"was just a drafter's situation.\" No amendments have yet been introduced to fix that drafting error. She said she was unaware of what happened with carpentry services and electricians' services, which make up a much smaller portion of the revenue that could be lost.\n\nLB 1 as introduced would also more broadly end some sales tax exemptions that multiple lawmakers who had been working with Pillen said were off the table:\n\nLB 1 is first up in a Revenue Committee hearing at 9:30 a.m. Tuesday. Two lawmakers on the Executive Board switched their votes Monday to send the bill there instead of the Government Committee.\n\nThe Revenue Committee, which Linehan chairs, is usually the designation for bills addressing taxation. Members could be more open to Pillen's proposal than those on the Government Committee, where only one of eight members supported a previous Pillen-backed proposal in the spring. {"uri": "8249664010", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-29", "time": "23:05:48", "dateTime": "2024-07-29T23:05:48Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-29T23:04:48Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.6823529601097107, "url": "https://omaha.com/news/state-regional/government-politics/record-breaking-81-bills-introduced-in-nebraskas-special-session-on-property-taxes/article_03cdbaae-4db4-11ef-b0d1-e3b5731a4c02.html", "title": "Record-breaking 81 bills introduced in Nebraska's special session on property taxes", "body": "LINCOLN -- Nebraska lawmakers introduced a record-breaking 81 bills, involving everything from legalizing marijuana to capping local officials' salaries, during the ongoing special session designed to consider ways to reduce the state's property tax burden.\n\nMonday was the final day senators could introduce new bills for the special session, which first convened Thursday. In total, the session produced 81 bills, plus 24 separate constitutional amendments, more than half of which just dropped on Monday.\n\nThat smashes the previous record of 54 bills introduced in a special session in 1935, back when the Legislature was still was bicameral, meaning it had a House and a Senate, and not unicameral.\n\nGov. Jim Pillen called lawmakers back to the Capitol to work to cut Nebraska's property taxes by 50% by the end of this year. His proclamation largely limits subjects of the session to tax-related issues.\n\nBut lawmakers' proposals for achieving property tax cuts run the gamut, from adjusting the state's homestead exemptions policy to regulating fantasy sports competitions.\n\nThe main portion of Pillen's plan, Legislative Bill 1, hit a snag on Friday when the Legislature's Referencing Committee voted to send the bill to the Government, Military and Veteran Affairs Committee, rather than the expected Revenue Committee. The switch would have removed some control from the bill's introducer, State Sen. Lou Ann Linehan of the Omaha area, to oversee changes to the bill. Linehan said she suspected the move was a covert attempt at killing the bill through sending it to a less friendly committee.\n\nThat issue resolved Monday when the Referencing Committee met again and voted to move LB 1 to the Revenue Committee. The decision was made after the committee received a request signed by both the revenue and government chairs, and was agreed to with little debate.\n\nLawmakers' ideas include tobacco, marijuana and ammunition\n\nHere are some of the 64 new proposals introduced Monday:\n\nTobacco. Two bills would increase taxes Nebraskans pay for tobacco products. LB 41, introduced by Sen. Jana Hughes of Seward, would increase the tax on electronic nicotine devices from 10% to 40%, and LB 60 from Sen. Justin Wayne of Omaha would establish a 20% tax on cigars, cheroots and stogies.\n\nPillen has proposed increasing the state's cigarette tax from 64 cents to $1.64 cents per pack as a way to increase revenues to offset property tax relief. Separately, Sen. Machaela Cavanaugh of Omaha suggested raising the tax to $2.14 per pack.\n\nCannabis. Two bills dropped Monday would legalize marijuana and forgive certain past convictions related to the drug, LB 52 from Wayne and LB 71 from Sen. Terrell McKinney of Omaha. Marijuana legalization has often been proposed as a way to increase state revenues, but such ideas have gotten little traction in the Legislature.\n\nElected official salaries. Certain elected members of Nebraska's political subdivisions, working full time, could not earn more than $36,000 per year, under LB 56. Introduced by Wayne, the bill limits the salaries of elected officials working in the legislative bodies of political subdivisions to no more than three times the annual salary of members of the Legislature. Lawmakers currently earn $12,000 a year. The bill would also limit part-time elected officials to no more than $24,000 per year.\n\nCurrently, Omaha City Council members earn $42,923 per year.\n\nFunding formula. Nebraska's current school funding formula, known as the Tax Equity and Educational Opportunities Support Act or TEEOSA, would be dissolved on July 1, 2027, under LB 81 introduced by Sen. Steve Halloran of Hastings. Lawmakers have attempted for years to propose alternate funding strategies in an effort to move Nebraska away from TEEOSA since its adoption in 1990, but none have proven successful.\n\nHalloran's bill would set a hard deadline to achieve the goal.\n\nLawsuits over taxes. Sen. Julie Slama of Dunbar introduced a collection of 10 constitutional amendments intended to prevent political subdivisions increasing the tax burden on its constituents without voter approval.\n\nPolitical subdivisions would be banned from increasing tax rates, levying new taxes, extending an expiring tax, changing tax policy in a way that results in net increases in tax revenue, or creating certain debts or financial obligations without having the necessary cash reserved. Slama's proposal would authorize residents to sue their political subdivisions if they take any of those actions without earning voter approval first.\n\nSlama also introduced a similar collection of constitutional amendments to require political subdivisions to save at least 3% of their fiscal year spending in reserve for declared emergencies, and limit annual spending increases for the state and political subdivisions to the inflation rate plus the change in population.\n\nAmmunition sales. A 5% excise tax would be imposed on ammunition sales under LB 65, which Wayne introduced. Half of the revenue from the tax would go to the state's property tax credit cash fund, and the other half would be split between two new grant programs the bill would establish -- one to support wildlife management, and the other to aid victims of post-traumatic stress disorder.\n\nResidential property. Valuation of residential property could not increase by more than 5% per year under LB 72, introduced by Sen. Mike McDonnell of Omaha. An accompanying constitutional amendment would classify residential real property as a distinct taxation class, allowing it to be taxed differently than other types of housing. Sen. Tom Brandt of Plymouth made a similar proposal this session for owner-occupied housing.\n\nEarlier in the session, lawmakers had proposed measures dealing with the governor's plane, reducing the prison population, legalizing online sports gaming and other measures to raise state revenues, reduce spending or limit property taxes.\n\nGovernment-politics Nebraska Gov. Jim Pillen's tax plan committee referral was 'a way to kill' it, lawmaker claims Erin Bamer , Martha Stoddard Government-politics Gov. Jim Pillen property tax plan", "body": "LINCOLN -- One former state senator contended Tuesday that Gov. Jim Pillen's plan to cut property taxes 50% by expanding sales and other taxes has support across Nebraska.\n\n\"The vast majority of Nebraskans are behind him in the struggle,\" said Dan Hughes, who farms near Venango. \"The property tax crisis in Nebraska needs to be fixed.\"\n\nBut opponents accounted for the vast majority of people who turned up to testify at a public hearing on Legislative Bill 1, which encompasses the key tax-related pieces of the governor's plan.\n\nNine hours into the hearing during the special session on property taxes, the Revenue Committee had heard from five times as many critics as supporters and many more opponents were waiting for their turns to speak. Combined, they called for changes in almost every provision of the sweeping proposal.\n\nLocal government officials issue warnings about the plan\n\nThe first portion of LB 1 would cap property tax revenues collected by cities and counties, bringing objections from a number of city and county officials.\n\nStephen Curtis, the City of Omaha's finance director, was among those who criticized the caps. He said they could hamstring Omaha's ability to cope with economic ups and downs or to deal with rising costs of city services. Although sales taxes may be enough to help out some years, they could fall short in others, he said.\n\nIn addition, he said lower property tax levies would hurt development projects funded through tax-increment financing, warning that the bill \"would jeopardize Omaha's positive momentum.\"\n\nDouglas County Attorney Don Klein said the looser caps proposed for hiring public safety staff should be broadened to include prosecutors and public defenders, while Gretna Mayor Mike Evans called for those caps to cover vehicles, computers and other equipment needed by public safety employees.\n\nSchool officials criticized the proposal to create a new property tax credit, worth $2.03 billion in the first year. The credit would be aimed at offsetting school property taxes while future lawmakers figure out how to fulfill Pillen's ultimate goal of eliminating property taxes for school operating costs.\n\nSpencer Head, president of the Omaha Public Schools Board, argued for keeping property taxes as a key source of school funding. He said property taxes offer stable funding for schools, while history has shown that state school aid is less reliable. He said state lawmakers have cut aid during economic downturns.\n\nEducation OPS board to vote on opposition to Pillen's tax plan in special Friday meeting Lauren Wagner\n\nLiz Standish, associate superintendent for Lincoln Public Schools, said that eliminating school property taxes would jeopardize local control because it would not allow school boards to not control local budgets. She also objected to the proposal to distribute the new property tax credit to taxpayers based on the valuation of their property, rather than school property taxes paid.\n\nTestifiers say Pillen plan could hurt adoptions, farmers, more\n\nThe governor's proposal to increase or impose sales taxes and sin taxes on more than 100 goods and services brought the most opponents, including farm groups, business groups and various affected businesses and professions. Under the bill, money raised from the tax changes would be used to fund the new property tax credit.\n\nMark McHargue, president of the Nebraska Farm Bureau, spoke for a coalition of agriculture groups, against putting a 2 cent sales tax on agricultural machinery and equipment. A later speaker said the proposal would drive people to other states to buy combines and other equipment, which can cost up to $1 million.\n\nOthers objected to higher taxes on liquor, home repair and maintenance, vehicle repair, veterinary care for pets, storage and moving services, pop, candy, advertising, manufacturing machinery and equipment, vaping, consumable hemp products, massage therapy, keno, games of skill, taxi services and legal services.\n\nAmong them, John Sennett, a Broken Bow attorney, said the proposed tax would have consequences for his clients. He said he doesn't want to have to tell a young couple that the cost of adopting a child just increased because of the new taxes.\n\nMelanie Kirk, executive director for the Nebraska Coalition to End Sexual and Domestic Violence, offered a hypothetical scenario showing how the sales tax expansion could affect people.\n\nThe story began with a woman, Laura, whose husband kicked down her door and disabled her car. Repair of both would now be subject to tax. Laura took an Uber -- newly taxed -- while her car was being fixed. Meanwhile, someone broke into her home and hurt her dog. Veterinary care for the dog would be taxed.\n\nThe next developments in the story had Laura paying tax for a rental agent to find a secure place to live, storage services before she could move in, a moving company, a new prepaid phone plan and finally collection services after her husband drained their bank account.\n\nPillen's plan had some fans as well. Deb Kelly of O'Neill said she knew the measure would require tradeoffs but she and her family would be willing to pay their fair share of new taxes to bring down property taxes.\n\n\"I'm just asking people to focus on what's good for the whole playground,\" she said.\n\nAnother proponent, Mark Beiting of Gretna, said his wife wants to keep their sons and grandchildren nearby but two sons are considering moving to Iowa because of high Nebraska property taxes.\n\n\"My wife begins to sob every time she hears this discussion,\" he said. \"You've got to take action.\"\n\nPhotos: Nebraska
